He rode a feathered carriage drawn by five dragons, adorned with variegated sashes, streamers, and banners before and behind, descending from the heavens. In an instant, he was introduced to Jing’s father and elder brother, then sent them away. He summoned Ma Gu, who reported having already received the summons to inspect Penglai and would depart at once, returning shortly. After two such intervals, when Ma Gu arrived, the sound of men and horses preceded her; her retinue was half the size of Fangping’s when she arrived, and Jing’s entire household beheld her. Though of fine years, she appeared no older than a maiden, her hair coiled atop her head with the rest cascading to her waist. Her garments bore patterns yet were neither brocade nor embroidery, their radiance dazzling like the sun, indescribable—all things unseen in the mortal world. She entered and bowed to Yuan, who rose to greet her. Each proceeded to the feast hall where delicacies were served, including unicorn jerky, before Yuan departed.

Jing’s parents privately questioned him, and he replied: “Lord Wang often resides on Kunlun Mountain, traveling to and from Mount Luofu and others. Upon those mountains stand palaces; when Lord Wang emerges, he rides but a single yellow unicorn, accompanied by a dozen attendants. With every journey, the spirits of mountains and seas all pay homage and bow in audience.” Yuan left a letter for Prefect Chen, its script expansive yet irregular, grand but uneven.

Previously, none knew Fangping’s name, but Yuan learned it thus. Chen Cun preserved Lord Wang’s handwritten letter in a small box. Later, Jing departed through *shijie*.

It is further said: Zhang Weizi was the daughter of Zhang Qing, the Grand Architect during Emperor Zhao of Han’s reign. Weizi loved the Dao and achieved *shijie*.

It is also said: Su Zixun was a man of Qi, yet none knew he possessed the Dao. In his village, he practiced trust and yielding, and over the years, his complexion never aged. When people pursued him, he vanished from sight. He often consumed elixirs, favored pure discourse, and habitually read the *Book of Changes* in leisure, composing texts all imbued with meaning. When the nobles of the capital heard of him, none failed to seek him with reverence, yet he could not be summoned. Later, when he happened to go out, the nobles’ carriages and parasols filled the road, and scholars reported that he had just departed—he was the one riding a donkey on the eastern path. Each spurred their horses in pursuit, but none caught up. Zixun arrived at Lord Chen’s home and said: “Tomorrow I shall depart and not return.” Lord Chen presented him with a single unlined garment of kudzu cloth. At the appointed time, Zixun died, dissolving into transcendence and ascending as an immortal.

It is further said: Yin Changsheng was a native of Xinye. Later, as a member of the Han imperial consort’s kin, he devoted himself wholly to the Daoist arts. Hearing that Ma Mingsheng had attained the Way of transcending the world, he sought him out. Mingsheng merely engaged him in lofty discourse day and night, discussing current affairs and the cultivation of fields and farming. Thus passed over a decade, and Changsheng never wavered. Of the twelve who studied with Mingsheng, all had departed, yet Changsheng alone grew ever more earnest. Mingsheng said: “You have truly attained the Dao.” He then led him into Mount Qingcheng and transmitted to him the *Scripture of the Grand Clarity Divine Elixir*. Upon completing the elixir, he ascended as an immortal. Changsheng composed nine scrolls, stating: “In high antiquity, immortals were many, but since the rise of the Han, forty-five have been recorded—including myself, six in total. Thirty achieved *shijie*, while the rest ascended in broad daylight.” Lord Yin’s preface reads: “In the first year of *Yanguang* of the Han, the disciple from the northern hills of Xinye received the essential secrets of the divine elixir from the Immortal Lord. Having perfected the Dao and departed the world, he entrusted them to the famous mountains.” Thereupon, Lord Yin tore yellow silk and inscribed the elixir scripture. One copy, encased in patterned stone, was placed on Mount Song; another, written on lacquered *huanglu* wood slips and sealed in green jade, was placed on Mount Taihua; a third, inscribed on golden slips and sealed in silver, was deposited on Mount Shuijing in Shu. One scroll, written on fine silk and compiled into a single volume, was entrusted to his disciples, to be transmitted through the generations. He also composed three poems to reveal to posterity.

It is further said: The Immortal Duke, named Wu Ding, was a native of Guiyang. During the Later Han, he served as a minor clerk in the county. Taciturn yet magnanimous, he was broadly learned in the classics, acquiring knowledge without a teacher, possessing a nature of spontaneity. Once, while on a mission to the capital, he returned via Changsha Commandery. Arriving too late to lodge at the postal station, he spent the night in the wilds.

Suddenly, he heard voices from atop a tree saying, "We are bound for Changsha to purchase herbs." At dawn, he looked and saw two white cranes. The Immortal Duke found this strange and followed them to the market, where he saw two men walking together beneath a white canopy. They said to him, "You are destined to become an earthbound immortal." They then instructed him to return home. Soon after, the Immortal Duke fell ill and died, his body undergoing liberation through simulated death.

It is further said: Long Bogao was praised by Ma Yuan, the General Who Pacifies the Waves of the Later Han, in his admonition to his brother’s sons as a man of exemplary virtue worthy of emulation. Later, Bogao received the method of ingesting fetal qi from the immortal Diao Daolin, and also the recipe for consuming the "Green Elixir," after which he became intoxicated and departed. He concealed himself on Fangtai Mountain, where the Master of Registers, Jun, ordained him. Bogao, whose given name was Shu, was a native of Jingzhao. During the Jianwu era of the Han, he served as the Magistrate of Xiandu and later as the Grand Administrator of Lingling. In his letter admonishing his brother’s son Yan, Ma Yuan wrote: "Long Bogao is sincere, circumspect, and prudent; his words are without fault. He is modest, frugal, impartial, and incorruptible, yet commands respect. I cherish and esteem him—may you and your peers emulate him."

It is also said: The wife of Cheng Wei, a Gatekeeper of the Han, was an adept who had attained the Dao and could master transformations. Cheng Wei pressed her to teach him her arts, but she refused. When he persisted relentlessly, she suddenly collapsed and died, her body undergoing liberation through simulated death and departing.

The *Inner Transmission of the Lady Wei of Nanyue* states: The Perfected One of Pure Emptiness, Wang Zideng, and the Azure Lad of Eastern Florescence descended together and bestowed upon her the formula "Hidden Transmutation: White Canopy Spirit Powder." They also gave her the "Spiritual Pill of Transformed Essence from White Stone and Refined Gold," instructing her to consume it all at once. She was to feign illness and remain secluded, for a fixed time had been set for their meeting at the Southern Yang Terrace of Luoshan, within the Yang Terrace Palace of Luoyang. With these words, the two Perfected Ones departed. She immediately took the medicine, then claimed a foot ailment, closed her eyes, and rested, drinking but abstaining from food. After midnight, the Supreme Unity and the Mystic Immortals dispatched a whirlwind chariot to fetch her, riding the qi and driving straight into her chamber. At that moment, her disciples attended her illness, and her family filled the room, yet none perceived what transpired.

The Southern Yang Terrace of Luoshan was where Yu of the Xia Dynasty once inscribed stone during his tour of the famous mountains. Below lies the Terrace of Radiance, where over ten thousand divine immortals and scholars gather.

It is also said: Lady Wang Jinxian was the daughter of Wang Yifu of the Jin Dynasty and wife to Crown Prince Minhuai. When Luoyang fell into chaos, Liu Yao abducted Jinxian, intending to take her as his wife. Jinxian cursed fiercely: "I am the wife of the imperial crown prince, the daughter of the Minister of Education—how dare a petty Qiang barbarian presume to defile me?" With these words, she cast herself into the river. Her maidservant, named Liuchu, also leapt into the river and perished. At that moment, the Perfected Woman of Mount Songgao, Han Xihua, was traveling nearby. She rescued them both, outwardly feigning their deaths while secretly conveying their bodies into Mount Songgao, where they now dwell within the Hua Yang Inner Grotto.

Liuchu was over twenty, refined in appearance and steadfast in virtue. She bore the surname Tian, hailing from Yuyang, and was the granddaughter of Tian Feng, former magistrate of Junyi during the Wei Dynasty. Feng’s hidden virtue extended even to Liuchu.

It is further said: Dong Feng, styled Junyi, was a native of Houguan. During the reign of the former lord of Wu, a young man served as magistrate of Feng’s home county. Seeing Feng, then over forty, the magistrate perceived no sign of his Daoist attainment and resigned his post. Over fifty years later, he encountered Feng again while traveling through Houguan in another official capacity. All the former officials and locals went to see him, yet Feng’s appearance remained unchanged from years past. He dwelled in the mountains without farming, treating illnesses without accepting payment. Those he cured were instructed to plant five apricot trees. Over the years, these grew to over a hundred thousand trees. He allowed people to exchange a vessel of grain for apricots, using the grain to relieve the poor and aid travelers in need, amounting to over twenty thousand *hu* annually. Eventually, he departed through *shijie* (corpse liberation).

Master Pei said: Those who attain *shijie* as immortals cannot ride the flowered canopy, mount flying dragons, ascend the Great Ultimate, or wander the Nine Palaces. Some, through the singular practice of the "Flying Essence Sword Liberation" at dawn, achieve the Eightfold Simplicity and Arrayed Records. Yet Dong Feng, though he treasured secrets and did not cultivate, still attained *shijie* in broad daylight. Others who employ water, fire, weapons, illness, or liberation through great swords or bamboo staves must first visit famous mountains, where they become *Taiqing* (Great Clarity) *shijie* immortals. All who practice the Way of Sword Liberation inscribe their names on purple slips, submitting them to the lofty immortals. Those with prior merit, virtuous deeds, hidden virtue, and faith in immortality may ascend to the Vermilion Fire Cinnabar Mound Palace to study the Dao and become Perfected Ones of the Nine Palaces. Those who employ the Great Ultimate *shijie* and depart at midnight serve as Earth Perfected. Depending on the depth of their cultivation, the steadfastness of their resolve, or their role as libationers refining essences or administering healing, they may become rulers of the Thirty-Six Grotto Heavens. Those who achieve *wenjie* (civil liberation) govern the underworld for 140 years before transformation, while *wujie* (martial liberation) ghost masters govern for 280 years before transformation. Only after three such ranks may they advance to immortal office.

The *Scripture of the Nine Heavens’ Divine Birth Chapters* states: Those who study the supreme Way, aspire to immortality, and attain *shijie* will ultimately achieve the Dao of immortality. Though their transformations differ, they do not intermingle, for all enter the perfection of the Way.

The *Bright Perfection Regulations* states: Those who in life love the Dao, refine their merit, and inscribe their virtuous names in the Upper Clarity may attain *shijie*. As lesser immortals, they wander the Five Peaks, later reincarnating among humans to receive scriptures and become masters of their kind.

The *Scripture of the Great Mystery* states: All who attain *shijie* must, according to the *True Regulations of the Four Poles*, wait 140 years before becoming Perfected Officials of the Divine Realm. Only then may they ride the flowered canopy, mount winged dragons, ascend the Great Ultimate, and wander the Nine Palaces.

The *Scripture of the Feminine Unity and Five Elders* states: When immortals depart this world, some vanish without trace, concealing their inner governance while appearing outwardly engaged; others leave behind swords and staves, drifting away like clouds and mist, their spirits lodged in mystery, their origins unknown. It also says: Those who possess this scripture of the Five Elders, even without fasting, meditation, or separating from the mundane, will not lose their hidden spiritual essence. They may attain *shijie* in broad daylight, pass through the Great Yin, or become underworld governors. Some leave behind bones or undergo womb transformation, receiving rebirth in the Southern Palace—all due to prior karmic affinity.

The *Scripture of the Upper Clarity* states: The Celestial Emperor of Primordial Beginning transmitted to the Southern Pole Primordial Lord seventy-four methods of bodily transformation and liberation from the Upper Clarity.

The *Inner Transmission of the Perfected of Great Clarity* and the *Records of Famous Mountains* state: The grotto of Mount Luofu spans five hundred *li*, located south of Kuaiji. Thirty *li* into the mountain, its peaks rise precipitously, where Ge Hong attained liberation.

The *Declarations of the Perfected* calls it Mount Zengcheng.

The *Collected Records of Immortals* states: Celestial Master Zhang Daoling secluded himself on Mount Longhu, cultivating the Way of the Three Primes’ Silent Audience. He obtained the Central Elixir of the Yellow Emperor’s Dragon and Tiger. After refining and consuming the elixir, he could divide his form and scatter his radiance. The Celestial Master entered Mount Songgao from Poyang and acquired the hidden scriptures of command and transformation. It further states: Zhou Yuanyou of Runan Ancheng was the daughter of Zhou Chang, governor of Henan during the Han. Chang’s lifelong hidden virtue extended to Yuanyou, who from childhood loved the Dao and consumed *fuling* (poria). Forty years later, she encountered Master Shi, who taught her the arts of vanishing and concealment, as well as the Way of Hidden Radiance. She departed through bodily liberation.

It is also said: Lu Meiniang of the Tang Dynasty was born with green eyebrows and a clever disposition. The governor of Nanhai presented her at court, where Emperor Shunzong marveled at her presence in the palace, calling her "Divine Maiden." She ate only a small measure of sesame rice. By the Yuanhe era, Emperor Xianzong admired her wisdom and bestowed upon her a golden phoenix bracelet for her wrist. Over time, she grew weary of palace life and was ordained as a Daoist priestess, allowed to return to Nanhai, where she was granted the title "Carefree." After several years of abstaining from food, she departed through *shijie*.

*End of Scroll Six of the Daoist Canon*
*Scroll Seven of the Daoist Canon*

**Sword Liberation**

*The Scripture of the Recording of Forms and Spirits by the Supreme Perfected Lord of the Great Ultimate’s Stone Essence and Golden Radiance* states: The method of forging the sword was originally transmitted by the Lord of the Western City, who governs all Perfected Ones, to the Purple Yang Duke, who practiced the Dao to completion. The Lord of the Western City also imparted the method of *sword liberation* and the *Ninefold Cyclical Elixir Formula* to Master Changli, who was a man of Zhou.

*The Preface of the Eastern Village* says: "Rinsing the dragon embryo to determine life and death, and ingesting the jade blossoms of the former master Wang of the Western City"—this refers to the practice of sword liberation after consuming the elixir, though it does not mention the *Ninefold Cyclical Elixir*. Though the formula was transmitted, it was not ultimately employed, and was later passed to disciples. Lord Mao also received it but did not use it, hence it was merely "passed on." *The Transmission of Lord Mao* records that Lady Wei of South Mountain transmitted it to Lord Yang, and the Consort of An remarked that one could seek the Way of Sword Liberation, though it is unknown whether it was ultimately practiced. As Magistrate Xu had already ingested *yushi* (fat stone), he may not have needed to pursue this method. However, his liberation by night in Zhenan followed a different path, for he was the son of Magistrate Xu. If this were *Great Clarity Liberation* or the use of the sword alone, he should not have been able to return to his homeland. Yet Yuan wandered freely in Fangtai and returned to his native land. When the divine sword is employed for liberation and transformation, one may roam and feast in the Great Ultimate, gather the numinous tracks of the Five Stars, and radiate the majestic light of the Seven Origins, shaping and reflecting the true qi with awe-inspiring brilliance. Thus, where are the swords buried by the Yellow Emperor on Mount Qiao? The household of the Prince of Bohai had a sword that cried out in an empty coffin; Wang Qiao’s tomb in Jingling had a sword that soared into the heavens—these are true proofs of the essence of the Nine Mysteries, responding to the numinous power of the Great Spirit, manifesting the wondrous transformations of the mighty sword.

Those who achieve *sword liberation* use the sword in place of the body. After five hundred years, the sword naturally returns to its original place. *Sword liberation* is not the only method. Those who employ cinnabar scriptures may also use an empty sword to produce eerie echoes, though such phenomena are elusive and difficult to trace, and cannot be spoken of. Those who cannot attain it may only perceive it in their hearts. The divine and marvelous are fleeting and formless, shifting without pattern, beyond the expectations of ordinary reason. One must follow the transformations with the heart and adapt to circumstances. Those who practice it do not perceive how it manifests, and those who witness it cannot fathom its workings.

The Yellow Emperor gathered copper from a hundred mountains to cast a cauldron, with tigers, leopards, and a hundred beasts tending the fire and assisting the furnace. When the Yellow Emperor suddenly passed, he was buried on Mount Qiao. Five hundred years later, the mountain collapsed, revealing his treasure sword and red slippers—yet one day, they vanished again. Wang Ziqiao once visited Mount Zhong, where he obtained *The Scripture of Nine Transformations and Ten Changes*, enabling him to conceal the sun and moon and wander among the stars. Later, he suddenly fell ill and died, and a tomb was prepared for him on Mount Bohai. During the reign of King Xiang of Xia, someone opened Wang Ziqiao’s tomb and found a sword resting on the northern couch, emitting a dragon’s cry—no one dared approach. Later, it too disappeared. Wang Ziqiao’s tomb in Jingling was opened again during the Warring States period, and a sword was seen. As someone reached for it, the sword suddenly flew away.

Accounts of Wang Ziqiao differ: some say Master Fuqiu carried him away on a crane to ascend Mount Song. In truth, he achieved liberation at the age of fifty-five or fifty-six, hence his wearing of the *Far-Ranging Traveler’s Cap*. Ge Hong once wrote that Lord Yin transmitted the method of *corpse liberation* to Bao Jing. After Bao’s death, he was buried at Stone Child Ridge. When someone opened his coffin, they saw a great blade, and heard the sounds of men and horses around the tomb—no one dared take it. This resembles the method of *sword liberation*, though the sword did not fly away. It was likely the *Numinous Treasure Great Mystery Yin-Sheng Talisman*, inscribed in cinnabar on the blade. Below the *Great Clarity* level, one merely attains life.

According to *The Numinous Transmission of Jade Clarity*, the Lord of the Western City wore a sword, and Lord Yang of Tongbai also wore one—these were likely cases of prior *sword liberation*. When a Perfected One employs a treasure sword for *corpse liberation*, it is a cicada-like shedding, the highest grade. The methods of *corpse liberation* are many, like the cicada’s molt. This sword, however, is not a molt, hence it is called the highest grade, signifying the loftiest path of transformation and concealment.

When forging the sword, one must first purify oneself for a hundred days. Then, in a secluded place near a clear spring, construct a west-facing chamber, with the furnace’s mouth also facing west. A skilled smith should refine fine iron, combining it with *shengdie* (raw iron) to forge a sword weighing eight *jin*. To attain its utmost essence, bamboo charcoal should be used. Copper and tin should be softened and blended, requiring much time and effort—thus, the ancients took three years to complete a sword. Xue Zhu said: "The Ouye smiths cast swords on the Red Bell Mountain, where tin was mined after breaking the rock; the Ruoye Stream was drained to extract copper." When these are combined and smelted, the metal is refined without drying, becoming hard, sharp, and malleable.

The smith must be gentle and kind, wearing new clothes and bathing. On the day of forging, he must not consume alcohol or meat, nor tread in filth. The sword should be made on the *gengshen* day of the seventh month or the *xinyou* day of the eighth month. Its length should be three *chi* and nine *cun*, its width one *cun* and four *fen*, and its thickness three and a half *fen*—this refers to the thickness near the hilt along the spine. Toward the edges of the blade, it may be reduced by one or two *li*. From the spine, taper two *fen* toward the edge, then shape the blade to the tip, gradually thinning it. The tip, nine *cun* in length, should be measured from the ring’s neck, with the spine carved to a width of three *fen* and eight *li*, ensuring the central ridge retains six *fen*. The tip, nine *fen* in length, should taper on both sides to a sharp point, forming two edges like a halberd. The hilt should be of a length that makes the ring two *cun* and four *fen* high, with the blade measuring two *chi* and four *cun*, leaving one *chi*, two *cun*, and six *fen* for the hilt. The iron tang should be centered, nine *fen* wide and two *fen* thick, connecting to the ring.

The hilt should be crafted from catalpa wood, with a circumference of four *cun* and nine *fen* near the ring and three *cun* and nine *fen* near the blade, both measured precisely by tapping to ensure accuracy. If fashioned as a sword, the grip should be flattened accordingly. The pommel may accommodate a greenish ring, forged from the initial refining of hard steel, ensuring the tang and blade remain seamlessly connected—never separately cast and joined with rivets. The overall form must maintain consistent dimensions in size and thickness, after which the hollowed interior is chiseled out. It may be easier to first drill several holes before carving. The base where the tang rests should be chiseled to a gentle curve.

Once complete, the main ring should stand two *cun* and four *fen* high, with its widest horizontal span measuring two *cun* and eight *fen* across the center. The inner square or circular opening should have a diameter of four *fen*. The forging styles of pommels vary across ages—some perfectly round, others elongated, or shaped like tripod handles—amounting to over ten distinct forms. The illustrations in this scripture merely demonstrate the bilateral symmetry of the design, not a fixed standard. Following ancient precedents, the diagram on the right presents a proportionate model.

The interior of the ring must not be perfectly round, like bamboo, nor perfectly square, like a ruler. Instead, the inner face should be slightly squared while the outer edges retain defined angles, ensuring the engraved blade characters remain upright without excessive slant. The total length is set at three *chi* and nine *cun*.

All measurements—length, breadth, thickness, and design—are fully specified. Refer to the accompanying diagrams, though note that ancient and modern *chi* differ in length. The *Nine Chapters on Mathematical Art* meticulously accounts for even the finest increments. Xun Kan, the Jin dynasty’s Minister of Central Affairs and a master of music and weaponry, lamented the discord of the eight sounds. Later, a jade pitch pipe from the Zhou era was unearthed, inscribed with its provenance and measuring four and a half *fen* shorter than the Jin *chi*. Using this relic, he recalibrated the musical scales, achieving perfect harmony. This scripture adopts the Zhou *chi* as its standard, for the Daoist scriptures of Zongtong and Tongbai were originally crafted with Zhou measurements.

The ring’s engravings—*lu huan*—involve carving the forged sword on both sides of the pommel. On the blade-facing side, nine "blade" characters (*ren*) are inscribed, while the reverse bears nine "already" characters (*yi*). The engravings follow the blade’s spine downward, aligning with the edges. The "blade" characters begin at the edge and descend toward the cutting edge, hence termed "following the blade." The carvings curve along the ring’s shape, thus called "following the ring." These incised grooves may be inlaid with gold or silver to enhance clarity and elegance.

The sword’s body bears symbolic engravings, and at the pommel’s center rises a small, ring-like protrusion called the *fuji* ("hidden base"). The *fuji* signifies the foundation of the sun and moon, concealing the radiance of autumn’s light. Within the inner ring’s left face, a "sun" character is carved, while the right face bears a "moon" character. The outer perimeter of the sun and moon is encircled by a border (*guo*). The inscription reads: *"Emerging after five hundred years, it sweeps the Five Peaks; concealed, it harbors no idle moments—veiling its spirit and concealing its form. Below, it governs the Nine Yin; above, it bears the Dark and Mysterious. It guards the feet to expel demonic forces, its might sufficient to reflect the Seven Luminaries. Uplifted, it mirrors the Five Qi; lowered, it transforms the body’s form."*

To distinguish the sword’s left, right, inner, and outer faces: hold the sword upright with the spine above and the edge below, pointing south. The eastern face is the right, the western face the left; the eastern side is the inner face, the western the outer face.

This sword’s dimensions—length, breadth, thickness, and engraved characters—serve as the model for the *Lingjian* ("Numinous Sword") of the Four Perfected of the Great Ultimate. The Perfected of the Highest Clarity also wield such swords, all forged by the Great Ultimate. Thus, this scripture presents the Great Ultimate’s method, established by the Four Perfected. Why insist on metal from Kunwu or jade-cutting blades? This clarifies that the essence lies not in refined iron but in the model itself. Wang Ziqiao’s sword was but common iron; the sword of his liberation on Mount Qiao was no Kunwu’s flowing gold.

This discourse was imparted by Lord Ziyang of the Great Ultimate Perfected to Lord Wang. When the Yellow Emperor ascended to the Dark Garden in a dragon-drawn chariot, his celestial retinue exemplified such transformations. This concludes the preceding chapter of the *Sword Scripture*.

The *Taiyi Shanghua Fu* ("Supreme Transformation Talisman of the Great Ultimate") is inscribed in flying script on paper, then placed within a purple cord and affixed to the right side of the sword’s ring. The *Quchen Feijing Shu* ("Flying Script of the Curved Dawn") is inscribed on the left face of the sword, extending to the blade’s base.

The *Taiyi Cangjing Fu* ("Concealed Radiance Talisman of the Great Ultimate") is inscribed in flying script on paper, stored in a crimson pouch, and tied to the right side of the scabbard when departing. The *Quchen Feijing Shu* is inscribed on the right face of the sword, extending to the tip.

The *Taiyi Luxing Fu* ("Talisman of the Register’s Form of the Great Ultimate") is inscribed in flying script on paper and worn on the day of departure, enveloping the body in seven-colored clouds and generating lightning. The *Quchen Feijing Shu* is inscribed on the sword’s spine, spanning both edges.

The *Taiyi Jiehua Fu* ("Liberation Talisman of the Great Ultimate") is consumed daily—one talisman for seven years—to achieve transformation. It is written in cinnabar on bamboo or silk, as recorded in the *Secret Essentials*. When the *Quchen* elixir is complete, inscribe it on the sword before liberation. After seven years, even a cinnabar-inscribed sword can effect transformation.

Tao Hongjing remarked: During the Yongjia era of the Jin, Liu Zeng (pronounced *mai*) possessed extraordinary knowledge and was said to be a transcendent. His sword, inscribed with Sanskrit-like characters and intricate designs, was forged in the manner of the Mahayana Dharma, its iron exceptionally sharp. In the Liu-Song dynasty, it became a standard imperial guard weapon, known as *Liu Zeng’s Blade* or the *Thousand-Ox Saber*. Over time, the engravings on the ring and banner faded. Several old rings, nearly identical, were observed, though the whereabouts of their blades remained unknown. Gu Jushang of Dongshan also crafted such swords, and some made by his contemporaries survive to this day.

Generally, deviations and errors are too numerous to enumerate in full, such as casting bronze into rings to rivet onto old blades. Such cases may be inferred by analogy. The present design, however, is solely the profound craftsmanship of Tao Hongjing, whose understanding is deemed supremely reasoned—though whether it aligns with the Great Ultimate’s model remains uncertain.

To test a blade’s sharpness, take several stalks of *zhongxing mang* and bind them tightly with a single hair, suspending the bundle from a staff held by one person. Strike once with the blade: if the stalks sever while the hair remains unbroken, the number of stalks cut determines the blade’s quality. Liu Zeng’s *Bo* and the *Thousand-Ox Saber* could sever thirteen stalks in old tests. Another blade, a hundred-times forged steel saber, cut twelve stalks, and was hailed as unmatched in the realm. Yet a recently crafted divine sword severed fifteen stalks. Its iron gleams with a deep azure sheen, its luster extraordinary—truly what Xue Zhu described as "melting like ice about to dissolve."

In recent times, over a dozen smiths have attempted such work, but none have matched this. The finest unadorned steel was forged by Xie Ping of Shangyu, while the intricate carving and mounting were executed by Huang Wenqing, a master artisan of the Imperial Workshops—both unrivaled in the land. In the first year of Jianwu of the Qi (494 CE), on the nineteenth day of the eighth month, construction began at Maoshan, continuing until the fourth year of Tianjian of the Liang (505 CE). The project was commissioned to craft ceremonial blades for imperial use, their refinement and beauty unparalleled in the world. A distinct method for forging transverse-hardened steel was developed. The imperial household also produced hundred-times forged blades, and Huang Wenqing was exempted from corvée labor, becoming a Daoist of the mountain abbey.

The *Rites of Zhou* prescribes a sword three *chi* in length, with a hilt of five *cun*—one-sixth of the total. The inner edge spans two and a half *cun* in width, weighing fourteen *liang* by the ancient scale (equivalent to three *jin* and ten *liang* by the modern measure). Modern imperial swords measure four *chi* and seven *cun*, with a hilt of one *chi* and five *cun*—slightly less than one-third of the total. The blade is one *cun* and six *fen* wide, though weight varies. This treasured sword is three *chi* and nine *cun* long, with a blade one *cun* and four *fen* wide. The classics distinguish between *dao* (saber) and *jian* (sword).

In the tenth year of Taishi of Emperor Wu of Jin (274 CE), the Minister of Works, Xun Xu, and Zhang Hua, among others, recalibrated the pitch pipes and eight sounds, finding them discordant with ancient music. The discrepancy arose from the gradual elongation of the *chi* since the Han. They thus reverted to the *Zhou Rites’* method of measuring millet grains to cast new instruments. Seeking ancient artifacts, they obtained a jade pitch pipe from the Zhou era, matching the old standard to the finest degree. Striking ancient bells, they tuned them by this measure, and all resonated in harmony. Seven other ancient artifacts were compared and found consistent. They also recovered bamboo slips from the Ji Tomb, each two *chi* and four *cun* long. Adopting this as the "ancient *chi*," they implemented it.

Ruan Xian, however, heard the music tuned to Xun’s pipes and deemed it excessively refined, not the sound of a flourishing state. After his death, someone unearthed a bronze *chi* from the ground, its length matching Xun’s measure—four and a half parts of the ancient *chi*. Given Ruan Xian’s expertise in music, the measure was reinstated as the "official *chi*," and the Ministry of Agriculture subsequently used it to standardize the five-*chi* measure and construct measuring vessels based on accumulated *cun*. During the Yuanjia era of the Liu-Song, the Grand General, Prince Pengcheng of the Imperial Clan, sought to enlarge objects and added half a *cun* to the official *chi*. The people adopted this measure, and it persists today as the "Imperial Workshops’ *chi*." The *chi* used by the Imperial Household, the Bureau of Waterways, and the Materials Office is one *fen* longer than the common measure. If one infers from these examples, the ancient *chi* would be...

The harmonies of pitch pipes and the five notes resonate naturally, aligning with the numinous and the unseen. During the Zhou era, two true masters of swordcraft, alongside Xun Xu and Zhang Hua, exhausted the utmost refinement in their work. Contemporaries such as Shu Xi and Lei Huan mastered the divine and the spectral. Though Ruan Xian excelled in music, his nature was unrestrained and eccentric, lacking precision—thus favoring the simple and slow. The unearthed bronze *chi* was likely a relic from the Later Han.

In the fourth year of Tianjian of the Liang (505 CE), the *chi* was recalibrated to rectify pitch pipes, adopting half a *fen* of Zhang and Xun’s ancient *chi*. This measure proved fitting and was implemented as the "standard *chi*."

It is further said: For those who wish to conceal themselves in famous mountains, observe transformations with the times, and, without seeking official transcendence, find contentment in reclusion, one should cultivate the Dao of sword-and-corpse liberation. Inscribe the *Quchen Feijing* script on both sides of the blade, first feigning illness as a pretext. Then, clutching the sword, depart outwardly—this is called "first falsely claiming illness." After resting outside for several days, secretly remove the sword from its green silk bag, draw it, and inscribe the talismanic characters and strike the tally. Once complete, hold the sword and recite the invocation, summoning the Heavenly Steed to arrive. Shed your garments and depart, ensuring no one perceives your transformation.

Other methods include stealthily applying the *Feijing* elixir to the sword’s ring and calling the sword’s name. After the invocation, the Great One will suddenly appear with the Heavenly Steed to welcome you before your dwelling. Mount the steed—if a woman, a curtained carriage will arrive. Since ancient times, transcendents have often used substitute objects: some employ bamboo staffs, others headscarves or shoes. Only Bao Jing utilized the *Taiqing* sword method. Such divine transformations are fleeting and illusory, assuming forms by coincidence, beyond rational explanation—truly wondrous.

The Heavenly Steed is the beast *Jiguang Teng Huang*. Ancient paintings and diagrams depict its form, passed down by former transcendents. *Jiguang* resembles a deer, *Teng Huang* a horse. Men ride singly, while women are conveyed in carriages. The Perfected of the Great Ultimate commands the Messenger of the Great One to lead the steed and hold the reins, presenting precious garments. They arrive in an instant, inexplicably.

The Great One presides over the transformations of transcendence. When the steed departs, though physicians and attendants surround the sickbed and descendants crowd the room, you may change attire, gird the sword, and vanish without detection—your actions now transformed. This is what is meant by "liberation through the three luminaries, lingering in the essence of the moon, breathing myriad changes, no longer in your former form."

It is also said: For the highest-grade liberation talismans, inscribe the *Feijing* script on paper and enclose it in a purple bag.

When you wish to depart, fasten the sword’s ring, conceal the *Lingjing* talisman inscribed in flying script on paper, and store it in a crimson bag. When you wish to depart, attach it to the sword’s scabbard. Many immortals use bamboo staffs, and not all necessarily master the sword method. Some may rely on Great Clarity techniques, employing objects for transformation—these cannot be sought in a single category.

It is also said: Those who practice the lesser *shijie* (corpse liberation) may never again gaze upon their homeland. The Way of the higher liberation is called "Matching the Purple Slip"; the Three Bureaus may no longer spy upon their dwelling. Simply keep the spirit-sword by your side in all daily activities. After thirteen years, you will naturally transform your form, without needing to rely on medicinal writings. If you cannot discern how to prepare the elixir, after seven years, merely inscribing the sword with cinnabar script will allow you to dissolve in concealment. Practicing this method alone, however, may still prevent you from returning to your homeland.

This falls short of the wondrous subtleties of *Quchen*, the swift conveyance of *Jiguang* and *Teng Huang*. Yet even in its singularity, it surpasses the miscellaneous methods of Great Clarity, enabling reshaping, refinement, and transformation, and allowing you to roam and feast in the Great Ultimate. Those who achieve *shijie* through

The *Taixiao Jing* states: "Those who practice the Great Dao are called *daoshi*." It further explains: "They devote themselves to the Dao, hence the title." During the reign of King Mu of Zhou, the Perfected One Yin Gui established the Louguan retreat, summoning reclusive worthies and appointing them as *daoshi*. When King Ping relocated the capital to Luoyi, he established seven *daoshi*. In the Yongping era of Emperor Ming of Han, twenty-one were appointed. Emperor Wu of Wei erected altars across the Nine Provinces and ordained thirty-five. When Emperor Wen of Wei visited Yong, he paid homage to Master Chen Chi and established fifty *daoshi*. Emperor Hui of Jin ordained forty-nine, granting them three hundred households.

The *Zhen’gao* records: Liu Xu, styled Zixiang, was a man of the Later Han. Born into wealth in Yingchuan, he aided the poor and served as governor of Chenliu. Later, he resigned his post and entered the mountains to become a *daoshi*. It also states: Chunyu Shen, styled Shuyi, was a native of Kuaiji. During the reign of Emperor Huan of Han, he served as a county magistrate before entering the mountains to cultivate the Dao. Further: Liu Kuan, styled Wenrao, was a prefect of Nanyang in the Later Han. At seventy-three, he entered Mount Hua and ingested cinnabar-dates. And: Wang Lang, styled Faming, was a native of Taiyuan. He entered Mount Mao and studied under Tao Hongjing. He transformed on the fourteenth day of the first month in the third year of the Datong era of Liang. Hongjing composed an inscription for him, offering libations with these words: "Crown and sash bring no glory; jade pendants are not treasures. A journey of ten thousand *li* in search of truth seals this inner embrace."

It further states: Tao Hongjing’s father, Tao Zhenbao, was eloquent and learned, skilled in cursive script, archery, and medicinal arts. Tao Hongjing himself excelled in clerical script, developing a distinct style though emulating Wang Xizhi. His essays and letters were highly esteemed. And: Sun Tao, styled Wencang, was a native of Shan in Kuaiji. He entered the mountains to study under Pan Siming, receiving the true methods and mastering calligraphy to a profound degree. Later, he studied Wang Xizhi’s script, imbuing it with deep meaning, and was celebrated in his time. The great stele of Nandong and the altar stele of Magistrate Xu were both his work. Tao Hongjing personally inscribed sutras and held secret oral instructions, which few disciples ever saw. Only Sun Tao and Huan Kaili, styled Kai, received them.

It also states: Zhu Zhong once sold pearls in Kuaiji during the reign of Emperor Gaozu of Han. He left a plain-text book with a woman named Ji while drinking wine. Ji secretly copied it and learned his arts. Further: *Daoshi* avoid attending funerals to preserve their spirit and prevent qi from dissipating. Thus, they withdraw from worldly service to preserve their essence. Though they fear harm to parents or teachers and must attend their funerals, such grief does no true injury.

The *Baopuzi* records: Xue Lü, styled Jihe, was a native of Yandai. During the reign of King Wu of Zhou, he studied the Dao north of the Zhong River. He failed seven trials due to indulgence and obstinacy. And: Guo Wenju, a native of Chaoxian in Henan, entered Mount Luhun to cultivate the Dao. Alone, he achieved a state beyond emotion and desire. And: During the reign of Emperor Da of Wu, a man named Li A from Shu lived in a cave without eating. He was seen for generations and called the "Eight-Hundred-Year-Old Elder." People sought his counsel, but Li A never spoke—only his countenance revealed answers. If he appeared joyful, the matter was auspicious; if sorrowful, ominous; if smiling, great fortune; if sighing, deep concern. His judgments were never wrong. One day, he suddenly vanished, and none knew his whereabouts.

And it is said: Fan Lingzi, from youth devoted to the immortal Dao, thus for many years. Later, he met Sima Jizhu, and together they entered the Chang Mountains. After seven years, they entered a stone chamber to the north. At its eastern corner stood a stone urn, or perhaps a stone window. Jizhu went out on a journey and earnestly warned him, saying: "Take care not to open it." Lingzi suddenly opened and looked inside. When Jizhu returned, he sent him away. Later, he summoned Lingzi again and had him guard a bronze coffer, once more warning him not to open it. Lingzi again opened it, and Jizhu then sent him away, so that he never attained the Dao.

And it is said: Feng Liang, a native of Nanyang. In his youth, he served as a county clerk, and at thirty became an assistant to the commandant and scribe. When sent to welcome the supervising inspector, he felt ashamed of his lack of ambition. He smashed his carriage, slaughtered his ox, tore and ruined his robes and cap, and left to study the *Book of Songs*, the *Commentaries*, the *Rites*, and the *Book of Changes* under a teacher. He also learned the Dao, divination, and the arts of observation. After fifteen years, he returned. The province and commandery honored him with appointments, but he declined. An imperial edict specially summoned him as a worthy and eminent candidate. Halfway there, he abandoned the journey and returned home. At sixty-seven, he fled the world, crossed eight mountains to the east, and dwelled in the Deer-Track Grotto.

It is further said: Anqiu Wangzhi, styled Zhongdu, was a native of Changling in Jingzhao. He cultivated and revered the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. Emperor Cheng of Han esteemed his virtue and power, often treating him as his master, yet Wangzhi humbly withdrew further. When the emperor summoned him, if Wangzhi was performing a liturgical rite, he would wait until the ceremony concluded before attending. The *Laozi Zhangju* preserves the scholarship of Anqiu, and when Wangzhi suddenly fell gravely ill, his disciple Gongsha Duyu stood beneath a garden tree. Wangzhi then awoke, fully recovered. It being winter, he detected the fragrance of plums; opening his eyes, he saw two crimson plums hanging on a withered branch. Wangzhi reached up to receive them, and the plums fell into his palm. After eating them, his afflictions vanished entirely, his body grew light, and his vision clear. He then departed, and none knew his whereabouts.

The *Daoist Biographies* states: Yan Ji, styled Zhongwei, lived during the reign of Emperor Ming of Han. From youth, he cherished the Dao and virtue, refusing office to wander among famous mountains. Later, he dwelled on Mount Wudang, resting without fixed abode—sometimes amid piled stones, sometimes leaning against great trees. His attire remained unchanged through the four seasons. He often wore his hair loose, though sometimes bound it with a silk headcloth.

It is further said: Bao Jing, styled Taixuan, was a native of Shangdang. A descendant of Bao Xuan, the Han dynasty inspector, he was by nature pure and wise, mastering the classics and histories. He cultivated himself and nurtured his nature, moving with such restraint that he never gave offense. Upon hearing of others’ faults, he reacted as if they had violated his family taboos. Many sought his instruction, and as he propagated the Dao and transformed beings, he was called the "Forest of Confucians."

It is further said: Wang Jia, styled Zinian, was a native of Longxi. He dwelled in a cave hewn into the cliffs of Dongyang Valley, his disciples numbering in the hundreds, each living in their own grotto. Though his appearance was coarse and unremarkable, he was keenly perceptive and witty. When asked about worldly affairs—good or ill—he never spoke plainly, yet his oblique answers often proved accurate.

It is further said: Yan Zun, styled Junping, was a native of Shu Commandery. He cultivated the Dao for self-preservation. When speaking to another’s son, he discoursed on filial piety; to a minister, on loyalty; to a younger brother, on deference—always seizing the moment to guide them toward goodness.

It is further said: Wang Yuan, styled Fangping, often descended to the home of Cai Jing. In an instant, Ma Gu arrived, her retinue half the size of Fangping’s.

Ma Gu’s fingernails were like those of a raven. Cai Jing thought to himself: *When my back itches, it would be fine indeed to have them scratch it.* Fangping said, “The Lady is a divine being—how can you entertain such thoughts?” He then had Jing flogged.

Jing wished to study the Dao under Fangping. Fangping made him stand with his back turned, then observed him from behind and said, “Your heart is crooked; you cannot be taught the immortal’s Dao.” Instead, he imparted to him the arts of transcending the world.

It is further said: Yu Chengxian, styled Chongguang, was a native of Yingchuan. Well-versed in the *Laozi* and *Zhuangzi*, he secluded himself at Whitewater Terrace in Wenjiang County. There he built a hut and lecture hall, where Confucians, Buddhists, and Daoists alike received his teachings. Later, he withdrew to the south of the Yangtze, declining repeated imperial summons. Eventually, he began lecturing on the *Daode jing* in Shixing, dissecting its knotty passages.

It is further said: Xue Yubao, styled Yanshi, was a native of Peiguo. During the Liang dynasty, he studied under Master Xuanpu, who admired his literary talent. Skilled in calligraphy, he once inscribed a passage on the wall of the Daoist Rectification Office in Chongling Abbey—all who saw it marveled at its excellence.

It is further said: Dongxiang Zong, styled Yilun, was a native of Qianzou in Gaomi. Once, while performing a ritual on an open-air altar, the incense in his casket was spent, yet it spontaneously refilled. Again, though no fire burned in his censer, fragrant smoke arose of its own, curling and suffusing the air without cease.

It is further said: Zhang Yu, a twelfth-generation descendant of the Celestial Master, established Zhaozhen Abbey and cultivated the fruits of renown, exhausting the mountain’s secluded charms. Emperor Jianwen of Liang composed an inscription for its stele.

It is further said: Qian Miaozhen of Jinling attained the Dao at Maoshan’s Wukou Grotto, and her disciples erected a stele for her on Maoshan. Prince Shaoling wrote a preface for the abbey, which remains extant today.

It is further said: In the second year of the Tianjian reign of Emperor Wu of Liang, offices for Greater and Lesser Dao Rectifiers were established. Meng Jingyi of Pingchang, styled Daofu, served as the Greater Rectifier at the time and frequently expounded the Dao for the state. In the fourth year, Prince Jian’an, Wei, inquired from his seat: “The Daoist scriptures and teachings impose strict prohibitions. The two chapters of *Laozi* are transmitted only after oaths and pledges—could it be that the intent of the ancient sages was not meant for ordinary discourse?” Jingyi replied: “To revere secrecy and uphold strict disciplines is the wondrous transformation of the orthodox tradition. Principle lies in mutual completion; affairs do not contravene one another.”

It is further said: Liu Faxian was a native of Pengcheng. At the time, Gu Huan had written *Exegeses on the Daoist Scriptures* for Kong Dezhang, offering many affirmations and refutations. Faxian sent him a letter to debate their points of agreement and divergence. Gu, yielding to the Dao, then inscribed: “I am but a remnant madman of antiquity, heedless of water or fire. Having received your admonition, I now begin to awaken. My past ravings are unworthy of further reproach.”

It is further stated: Whenever Faxian witnessed disputes between Daoist and Buddhist adherents, he composed the *Treatise on Quelling Contention*. Gu Huan, in turn, wrote the *Debate on Barbarian and Chinese Ways*. At times addressing the three teachings, he elucidated the similarities and differences between Buddhism and Daoism.

It is also said: Zhang Tan, a native of Jiaxing in Wu Commandery, was adept in profound discourse. He frequently expounded the *Laozi*, cultivated the supreme Dao, and studied the highest scriptures. People gathered from afar to attend his lectures. Tan sought true esoterica and deeply understood the orthodox tradition.

It is further said: Chen Jingshang, a man of Wu, excelled in recitation and was unmatched among both Daoist and Buddhist practitioners. He composed the *Scripture of Numinous Writ*, which circulated widely in the world. The Prince of Shaoling of Liang held him in great esteem, summoning Jingshang to accompany him to Ying. Jingshang eventually passed away in Jiangxia.

It is also said: Huan Kai, styled Yinshu, was a native of Dantu in Donghai. At the beginning of the Liang dynasty, three ancient lacquered bamboo containers were discovered on a sandy islet of Kunlun Mountain. Within them were yellow silk scrolls transcribing the three sections of the *Scripture of Great Peace* revealed by Lord Qian. The villagers, astonished by the scripture’s origins, reverently enshrined it and first presented it to Tao Yinju. Yinju declared: “This is the authentic ancient text of Lord Qian.” Upon hearing that the scripture was to be brought to the capital, he fell gravely ill with malaria, unresponsive to all treatments. Tao Yinju remarked: “This affliction is not of my doing. I fear the fault lies in taking the scripture away. Why not return it to its original place?” They complied, and after the scripture was sent back within two days, he recovered.

It is further said: Cao Bao, styled Shizhen, was a native of Danyang. Skilled in performing the *Step of Emptiness*, his frost-white hair and distinguished bearing surpassed all others. Those who encountered him on his travels universally praised him.

It is also said: Yan Zhiming, styled Huishi, was a native of Jinling. By nature, he possessed a fine voice, and from childhood, he excelled in recitation and chanting. When Emperor Ming was ill, he often summoned the Daoist assembly to the inner palace to perform the *Way-circling* ritual. Upon hearing Zhiming chant the scriptures, the emperor was deeply pleased, saying his illness had been alleviated. After the ritual concluded and Zhiming withdrew, the emperor, restless in the dead of night, urgently summoned him to recite before the throne. At once, he felt joy and relief.

It is further said: Xu Shizi, styled Dewei, was a native of Donghai. When Emperor Wu of Chen established the Zongling Grand Abbey, he appointed Dewei as its abbot. After Dewei’s passing, Emperor Wen reproached the officials responsible for funerary provisions and personally oversaw his burial.

The *Scripture of Great Peace* states: Yan Jizhi, styled Jingchu, was a native of Jurong in Danyang. A Daoist priest, he served his parents with utmost filial piety. Residing at Shizhu Abbey, he dared not leave his aged mother. Instead, he welcomed her to the abbey, building a small dwelling nearby to attend to her needs. After his mother’s death, he mourned to the point of emaciation beyond ritual propriety, earning the admiration of those who knew him.

It is also said: Du Xi, styled Fanghui, was a native of Jinxiang in Gaoping and served as General of Pacification for Jin. His heart inclined toward the Daoist teachings, which he secretly practiced. Skilled in clerical script, his calligraphy rivaled that of the Right Army.

He personally transcribed Daoist scriptures by hand, amassing nearly a hundred scrolls, many of which survive to this day.

It is also said: Zhang Xiaoxiu, admiring Wang Yuan’gui’s exquisite calligraphy, repeatedly invited him to visit. Yet Yuan’gui, enamored of springs and rocks, spent his days playing the zither, whistling, and chanting, never once taking up his brush. Only upon departing did he write a few lines in running script. Xiaoxiu, ever respectful, dared not press further. Traces of his calligraphy remain in the Abbot’s Hall of the Jianji Abbey.

It is also said: Xu Sigong was the younger brother of Xu Yuanyou. From birth, he was drawn to the Dao, renowned for his refined and scholarly demeanor. Emperor Jianwen of Jin counted him among his transcendent friends.

It is also said: Ren Dunshang, a native of Bochang, during the Yongjia era, attended a Daoist lecture on Mount Mao and gathered with the assembly. Dunshang sighed in secret: “Though many claim to admire goodness, their devotion is but superficial. I have yet to meet one whose heart is true enough to cleave metal.”

It is also said: Lu Na, Minister of Works during the Jin dynasty, fell ill with sores at the age of forty. He told Du Gong: “In every generation, those who die young suffer from these sores at life’s end.” Gong submitted a memorial on his behalf and presented him with *Cloud-Flying Powder*, saying: “Your destiny extends to seventy.” The prediction proved true. When the Right Army General Wang fell ill, he summoned Gong. Gong remarked to his disciples: “If the Right Army’s illness does not improve, what need has he of me?” Ten days later, Wang indeed passed away.

It is also said: Jiang Fuchu, a native of Yixing, shared a transcendent friendship with Xue Biao of Jinling. Coming and going on Mount Mao, he harbored aspirations of ultimate commitment. During the Yongming era of the Qi, he briefly descended to the capital, where Tao Hongjing, upon their first meeting, recognized their profound affinity. Later, when Tao resigned his office and built a hermitage in the central peaks of Mount Mao, he invited Fuchu to cross the ridge and join him in studying scriptures, medicinal arts, and alchemy, often engaging in discourse together.

It is also said: Yang Chao, styled Chaoyuan, was a native of Donghai. He served Tao Hongjing, accompanying him on his journeys and debating with him, earning the privilege of being counted among his closest disciples.

It is also said: Zhu Huikai, styled Zhiyuan, was a native of Wucheng in Wuxing. He devoted himself to precepts and self-cultivation, making relief and salvation his duty. In the eighth year of the Daming era of the Qi, when famine struck the land, Huikai distributed all his stored grain to the needy, and his village relied on him for survival. Three starving men, having gorged themselves, died of overeating. Their families sued the county, accusing Huikai of feeding the hungry to death and slandering him bitterly. The magistrate laughed and said: “To die from overeating after begging for food, then blame the host—there is no law for such a case.” He dismissed the matter without further inquiry.

It is also said: Pu Yang, of unknown origin, was wholly devoted to the Dao, and his prayers were always efficacious. When Zheng Xianyin’s daughter was lame, Yang treated her, and she soon recovered. When Emperor Jianwen of Jin deposed his heir and had no successor, he sent someone to pray to Yang. That very night, a yellow vapor arose from the southwest and descended into the palace chambers. At that time, Empress Li conceived Xiaowu.

It is also said: Xu Mai, styled Shu’xuan, was originally named Ying and later changed his name to Yuanyou. Resolved to seek the Dao of immortality, he entered the western mountains of Lin’an and did not return for months, nor did anyone know his whereabouts. He had previously married the daughter of Sun Hong, Cavalier Attendant-in-Ordinary of Wu Commandery. While dwelling in the mountains of Lin’an, he wrote a letter to dismiss his wife, saying: “I long to hear the resounding thunder and the cries of mountain birds, to compose for myself the *Nine Songs of Shao* upon the flute—yet I cannot bear it. Beneath the verdant canopy, dwelling in a cavern’s chamber, I find a palace or vast hall cannot surpass this. My heart’s desire is to end my days here; my resolve is fixed upon this place. I must depart—this is our eternal farewell.”

Chu Boyu, styled Yuanqu, was a native of Qiantang in Wu Commandery. From an early age, he admired the Dao of emptiness and detachment. At eighteen, when his father arranged a marriage for him, he entered through the front gate only to depart through the rear, making his way to Mount Kuaiji, where he dwelled on Waterfall Mountain. His nature was such that he endured both cold and heat, remaining in the mountains for over thirty years, utterly cut off from human affairs.

Another account states: Boyu first traveled to Mount Heng in the south, then journeyed into Min, where rushing torrents and perilous paths awaited. One evening, as he moored his boat by the river’s edge, a sudden gale arose, and the mountain waters surged violently, hurling his vessel to the summit before it crashed down a sheer cliff. His companions, believing him crushed like ice, searched in vain—only to find Boyu composed, guiding the boat with a small staff across unfathomable depths. The people of Wu were struck with awe and reverence. He later departed for Mount Huo.

Initially secluded on Waterfall Mountain, Emperor Gaozu of the Qi Dynasty admired his character and wished to meet him. Boyu declined, pleading illness, and departed. The emperor later regretted this, and by imperial decree, the Taiping Abbey was established at the foot of Waterfall Mountain, with Kong Zhigui erecting a stele in commemoration.

Zhang Ling was broadly learned, mastering the celestial patterns of the Yellow and Luo Rivers in all their subtlety. Dwelling in quietude beneath his humble gate, he sought neither fame nor advancement. He played the zither, chanted poetry, and followed his inclinations—nothing more.

The Venerable Dragon Might was a mountain-dwelling adept of the Dao. No one knew his true name; he was simply called the Recluse of the Mountains. Aloof and unyielding, he stood apart from the world of men.

Tao Hongjing, styled Daoming, was a native of Pingyang in Wei Commandery. He styled himself the Recluse of Huayang and often said to others: “My mind is ever like a mirror suspended—nothing that touches it escapes reflection.” He delighted in performing hidden acts of virtue, aiding the distressed and impoverished, and compounding medicines upon which both near and far depended. He never slept during the day and read until midnight without fail. Fond of the sound of wind in the pines, he abstained from rich and fatty foods. In his later years, he consumed only water shield, laver, and raw ginger, and though he could drink a *dou* of wine, he never became intoxicated.

*The Sacred Records of the Old Master* states: The divine physician and immortal of Mount Song, Cheng Gongyu, ascended to the Dao on the sixth day of the seventh month in the fifteenth year of the reign of Yao Hong of the Jin. His disciples wished to bury him with full honors, but Cheng suddenly revived and said: “A Daoist is free of worldly burdens and differs from the common folk—why mourn and bury me with such extravagance? Simply perform the rites of purification and offering; this accords with the ancient ways of the pure and true.” With these words, he departed. The next day at noon, someone knocked at the stone chamber. When the disciples opened the door, they saw two youths leading them inside, where Cheng rose and departed with them. He was buried in the Pure and Plain Hamlet of Gong County.

Meng Daoyang, styled Xiaoyuan and also known as Yuan, was a native of Pingchang. In his youth, whenever he heard of a gathering to expound the Dao, he would travel great distances to attend, regardless of the journey. As he grew older, his nature became serene and inward, his studies pursued for his own sake, not for recognition. He shut his door, opened his window, and pored over ancient texts. When he entered his chamber to read aloud, his voice barely escaped the room—yet when Liu Huan and Dai Shuo visited to discuss the profound principles of the Dao, they marveled and deferred to him, acknowledging his surpassing insight.

Wu Meng, styled Shiyun, was a native of Yuzhang. His nature was one of pure filial devotion: on summer nights, when at his parents’ side, he dared not swat away mosquitoes or gnats, fearing they would leave him only to alight upon them. At thirty, a man of the village, Ding Yishi, who followed the Dao, transmitted his arts to him. When the villagers of Yintong prepared wine for him, he departed, and the wine in the vessels remained undiminished. A Daoist named Shu Daoyun suffered from malaria that year, but Meng taught him the *Three Sovereigns’ Poems* to recite, and he was instantly cured. Once, returning to Yuzhang, he drew upon the river with a white feather and crossed it. To the east of the county stood a stone bamboo shoot, which had never opened through the ages.

Wu Meng went to open it and found many bamboo slips and documents, their ancient script indecipherable. South of the county stood a towering rock, rising a thousand *ren* into the air, too sheer even for gibbons to scale, yet Meng, staff in hand, ascended it. The magistrate of the county, Xin Cai Qianqing of Xincai, was fond of hunting, and though Meng repeatedly admonished him, he would not listen. Later, when Qianqing staged a great hunt, surrounding the land with fire to scorch the sky, Meng sat calmly amid the grass, birds and beasts gathering around him, the flames unable to reach him. Qianqing was deeply shaken and repented. When Wang Dun seized Meng during a gathering, he suddenly vanished, enraging Dun. That same year, Dun met his downfall. Meng ascended Mount Lu, where he saw an old man sitting beneath a tree, offering him sweet dew in a jade cup. There were also jade chambers and golden halls, where several figures spoke with Meng as if they were old acquaintances, serving him jade nectar throughout the day. Meng then rode an iron-red steed atop Mount Lu.

It is further said: Qian Miaozhen was a native of Jinling. From childhood, she was drawn to the Dao and wished to leave the worldly life, but her family forced her into marriage. Weeping, she steadfastly refused, and so she took residence on the Greater and Lesser Mount Mao. Later, she journeyed to Wukou Cave, where she personally transcribed a letter and seven poems for Tao the Recluse.

It is further said: Kong Lingchan was a native of Shanyin in Kuaiji. When his mother passed away, his filial devotion became widely known. From that time on, he abstained from fine foods and rich wines.

He subsisted on gruel and vegetables, clad in plain cloth, and maintained this resolve for the rest of his life. When his father saw him emaciated from grief, he was moved with pity and ordered a meal prepared. Lingchan reluctantly obeyed his father’s command, but swallowing the food made him ill. His father, recognizing that a person’s heavenly nature could not be altered, ceased pressing him further. Lingchan delved deeply into the subtleties of the Dao and perused all the scriptures of immortality. Emperor Ming of the Song dynasty established the Huaixian Abbey beside Yu’s Cave and decreed that Lingchan reside there. He was later promoted to Grand Master for Splendid Happiness and granted the title Gentleman of the Palace. Emperor Gao bestowed upon him a deer-hide cap, a gibbon-fur robe, and bamboo vessels of purity, with a handwritten edict that read: *"You possess the bearing of the ancients; I grant you the attire of a recluse. On the day of your ascent or voyage, I shall remember you."*

It is further said: Zhang Yi, styled Shihe, was a native of Wu Commandery. He encouraged his disciples, upheld the discipline of ritual affairs, and frequently expounded the sutras to assemblies, his reasoning profound and meticulous, his words refined and eloquent—revered by all in his time. Emperor Wu of Liang held him in high esteem. When Tao the Recluse composed the *Treatise on Dharma and Precepts*, clarifying the two teachings of Buddhism and Daoism, Yi engaged in repeated discussions with him, presenting well-ordered arguments, for which the Recluse praised him. Devoted entirely to the Dao and its methods, he lived in poverty yet remained steadfast, excelling in the eight styles of calligraphy and creating a unique script called "cloud seal." He composed the *Stele of the Southern Grotto of Mount Mao*, which was highly refined.

It is further said: Song Wentong, styled Wenming, was a native of Wu Commandery. During the reign of Emperor Jianwen of Liang, Wenming, having elucidated all the Daoist scriptures, compiled the *Commentary on the Meaning of the Lingbao Scriptures*, titling it *The Gateway to Understanding*. He also composed *The Great Meaning*, naming it *The Abyss of Meaning*, which scholars relied upon, and he was invited from all directions. Skilled in writing yet reticent in speech.

It is further said: Wang Suiqi, a native of Taiyuan, served as the abbot of Jizhen Abbey. By nature, he slept little; even when deeply asleep, he remained aware of others’ conversations. If discussions touched upon relevant matters, he would grasp their words at once, and all marveled at his clarity and wakefulness.

It is further said: By day and night without ceasing, the sun and moon merge their light.

**The Eight Boundaries of the Daoist Canon**  
**Section Nine: Fasting and Purification**

The *Scripture of the Most High* states: Contemplating the spirits, recording the qi, practicing the methods, and ascending the steps—these are called purification. The Perfected of the Great Ultimate says: No purification surpasses that of the *Lingbao*; its methods are lofty and wondrous, not proclaimed to the world. The oral instructions of immortals and sages are secret and unwritten. The purification rites of the Great One are profound beyond profundity.

The *Scripture of Guidance* states: To follow the Dao without purification is like walking at night without a torch—one strays from the Way and suffers needlessly. The *Canons of the Great Perfection* states: The purification of the Three and the Guarding of the One form the foundation; the study of the Dao takes purification as its root. What the Most High values, what the Old Master cultivates, and what the immortals and perfected rely upon.

It is further said: If a Daoist can eat but once at midday, avoiding all that is foul and impure, and observe purification in accordance with the lunar cycle, ceaselessly by day and night, harming nothing—delivering even worms and flies—then the supreme Dao shall be attained.

It is further said: When Daoists study the scriptures and practice their arts, they must first take up the two chapters of the *Dao and Virtue*, receiving them through purification to deliver others and save themselves.

It is further said: To relieve the illnesses of one’s parents, one must purify in the open-air terrace of the abbey. During purification, present the *Midday and Midnight Petitions*; sincerity will surely move the spirits.

It is further said: The Dao of the Jade Emperor of Mysterious Clarity was first transmitted by the August Emperor of the Highest Heaven to the Lord Emperor of the Grand Tenuity, the Three Origins Lords of Purple Essence, and the Perfected Lord of Primordial Yang. Later, it was passed to the Most High Lord of the Dao, who conveyed it to Lord Li of the Golden Watchtower. Lord Li transmitted it to the Perfected of Great Spirit, the Red Lord of Southern Marchmount, and the Lady of Purple Origin, who in turn passed it to Lord Wang of Pure Spirit. Lord Wang transmitted it to the Lady Wei of Southern Marchmount, who conveyed it to Lord Yang, instructing him to bestow it upon Director Xu and his disciples. Those receiving it must purify for seven days.

It is further said: When the host of perfected transmit the Dao, one must first purify for a hundred days, or thirty days, or ten days. One must also announce the purification a day in advance to cleanse the pure heart. Among the scriptures sought through six transmissions over three thousand years, none surpass this in purification. Practicing it for fourteen years, the Most High shall welcome one with a jade carriage, ascending to the Highest Clarity as a perfected being.

The *Scripture of the Three Sovereigns* states: In all purification, three matters are addressed to settle the deeds of heart, mouth, and body. It is further said: In all purification, one recites and studies the primordial registers and the great scriptures, wearing the inner texts to perfect the Dao. It is further said: In all purification, one wears the inner texts of the Three Sovereigns to perfect the Dao of concealment and manifestation.

The *Hidden Instructions for Ascending to Perfection* states: The Daoist purification is called "guarding stillness," meaning to settle the heart in purification, purify the body in cleanliness, and still the spirit while dismissing affairs—this is the purification of the inner heart that separates inner and outer. It is further said: Those who cultivate the Dao must purify and offer rites to atone for the transgressions of seven generations.

It is further said: The Highest Clarity gathers the Five Perfected on auspicious days. All who cultivate the Dao should, on such days, contemplate virtuous deeds in their hearts, aspiring to become flying immortals, establish virtue, bestow favors, and relieve the destitute—these are the affairs of the Most High. One must purify, dismiss all distracting thoughts, and seclude oneself in a quiet chamber.

The *Declarations of the Perfected* states: Purification must not be practiced in mixed company, for it will surely disrupt the true Dao. On days of *jiayin* and *gengshen*, when corpse-ghosts vie in chaos and the spirit is restless and defiled, one must purify without sleep, dismissing all desires. On the five *mao* days, one should always purify, enter the chamber facing east, and bow in the heart while visualizing the qi, expressing one’s intentions as desired.

It is further said: During the *Jian’an* era of the Han, Zuo Ci heard that Mount Juqu in Jinnling connected to Mount Emei and Mount Luofu, so he crossed the river to seek it. After purifying for three months, he ascended the mountain and found its gate. Lord Mao bestowed upon him three kinds of divine mushrooms, along with the nocturnal liturgy, saying: "Fragrant maidens bearing incense shall descend to protect those who purify."

It is further said: The Perfected of the Great Ultimate received the oral instructions of the Most High. After five transmissions over a thousand years, following the rules of the hidden texts, one must purify for five days before receiving the covenant.

The *Pearl Satchel* states: When entering the Five Marchmounts, one must bathe on the first *jiazi* day of the month, purify for seven days, and then proceed. If the sacred mountains bear divine mushrooms or rare herbs, one must present a plain memorial in cinnabar script. Its words should petition for ascension as an immortal, deliverance from the world, and flight to the Highest Clarity, bowing twice. Release a rooster and a dog upon a stone and depart; then seek the divine mushrooms and rare herbs, and all desires shall be fulfilled. Evil ghosts and ancient specters shall not dare to test one. Those who study the Dao yet know not this protection will encounter many calamities in the mountains.

The *Master Embracing Simplicity* states: If a household possesses the *Charts of the Three Sovereigns*, one must first purify for a hundred days before summoning the spirits of heaven, earth, the Five Marchmounts, and the altars of soil and grain. Later, the sagely lords commanded the Perfected of Lesser Clarity to compile the *Records of the Upper Perfected*, collectively titled *The True Canon of the Highest Perfected*, to subdue all demons. It is transmitted to others once every hundred years, and one must purify to receive it.

The *Scripture of the Three Origins* states: The foundation of studying the Dao lies first in cultivating the Middle Origin. The methods of purification atone for sins and repent transgressions before the Great Perfected, inscribing one’s name in the *Mysterious Chart*.

The *Red Writ of Lingbao* states: The Five Elders, the Emperors of the Heavens, and the August Thearchs gather in the six purification months of the year at the Numinous Capital Palace of the Three Upper Heavens, upon the Terrace of Primordial Yang in the Purple Tenuity, to calculate the celestial origins and examine the movements of destiny. It is further said: The old statutes of the Purple Tenuity Palace are written in cinnabar on white silk; one must purify for a hundred days to recite them.

The *Scripture of the Jade Register of Great Simplicity* states: Through purification, visualization, and recitation of this scripture with unceasing diligence, one shall attain spiritual penetration and wisdom, becoming a Perfected of Great Simplicity.

The *Scripture of Great Peace* states: The Perfected say: The human spirit constantly dwells in empty and tranquil places, not in filth and turbidity. Those who wish to recall the spirit must purify in a fragrant chamber, and all illnesses shall vanish. Without purification, the spirit will not return to the person. All shall be reported to heaven, which is why human illnesses accumulate and the dead never cease.

The *Scripture of the Eight Simplicities Racing Through the Heavens* states: Observe the Seven Luminaries and Five Planets, discerning their forms and colors, noting their early or late appearances while awaiting them in the courtyard. On dark and overcast evenings, visualize them within the bedchamber, maintaining purification and cultivation as in the method of visualizing the racing sun and moon. The crimson qi with purple rays at midday is the radiant splendor of the Nine Spirits of the Luminous Effulgence. The yellow qi and white light within the moon, with the eightfold brilliance of clarity, are the nocturnal essence of the Moon’s Splendor, known to all Daoists. By absorbing the qi of the sun and moon and drawing in the ninefold radiant effulgence, one may cultivate the Way of visualizing the racing sun and moon. The Mystic Consort bestowed upon Lord Yang two scrolls of wondrous methods. The Way of the sun and moon is profoundly lofty and subtle.

The *Numinous Writ of the Golden Portals of Supreme Clarity* states: The Auspicious Assembly Day of the Most High is the day when the Perfected of the Highest Realm gather to celebrate and offer tribute. The Day of Returning to the Origin is the day of the Most High’s renewal. The *jiazi* day marks the inception of yin and yang’s primordial qi, and the lunar dark-moon phase heralds its transformation. It is the dawn of a new day. Those who do not know this day may neither absolve sins nor engrave bamboo slips. The Perfected must purify and fast, visualizing auspicious matters on this day—this is the method of Returning to the Origin.

It further states: The *Scripture of the Inner Radiance of the Yellow Court* is also called the *Writ of the Zither-Heart of the Most High* and the *Golden Book of the Great Emperor’s Supporting Leaves*. The courtiers of the Great Emperor’s palace recite this scripture in its entirety. It is inscribed on golden tablets, hence called the *Golden Writ*, and is also known as the *Jade Chapters of the Eastern Flower*. The Eastern Flower is the name of the Palace of the Square Cauldron, the abode of the Blue Lad of the Eastern Sea. Within it, the immortals’ courts often purify, fast, recite, and chant, inscribing jade tablets.

It further states: The *Verses of the Left Perfected of the Great Ultimate on the Mysteries of the Curved Simplicity* is also called the *Mysterious Mound Writ of the Nine Heavens’ Phoenix Qi*. The Most High bestowed it upon the Left Perfected of the Great Ultimate, who transmitted it to the Palace of the Square Cauldron in the Eastern Sea. The Blue Lad, Great Lord, commanded that it be passed to Daoists of predestined fame who are destined to become Perfected Immortals. After wearing it for twenty years, one may behold the Three Primordial Lords and fast by the oath of azure gold, replacing the covenant of alliance.

The *Secret Words of the Eight Ways* states: Those who wish to practice the method of the Nine Perfected must purify and fast in a quiet chamber, focusing their minds in solemn prayer to ascend as flying immortals to the Purple Court. It further states: The Phoenix Qi of the Nine Heavens, the Writ of the Mysterious Mound, the Elder of the Dark Capital, and the immortal statutes of transmission and wearing—after purifying and fasting for three days, those who wear it attain immortality.

The *Preface and Methods of the Scripture of the Way and Virtue* states: Yin Xi, perceiving the purple qi advancing westward, purified and fasted in anticipation of beholding the True Dao. When Laozi crossed the pass, he bestowed the two-chapter scripture upon him.

The *Scripture of the Numinous Writ* states: The Ten Divisions of Wondrous Scriptures, inscribed in gold on jade tablets, are recited by the Perfected Immortals of all heavens who purify and fast, ascending on appointed days to the Jade Capital to chant their texts.
